Ibn Sīnā's Proof on Celestial Bodies' Temperature
Ibn Sīnā proves celestial bodies are not hot/cold via correlation, with objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Sīnā proves that celestial bodies are neither hot nor cold, and how he defends his argument against the objection that the correlation is not universal.

Core Idea
The correlation of heat/lightness and cold/heaviness is contingent on receptive matter.

Ibn Sīnā uses the observed correlations in sublunar elements to argue that celestial bodies, lacking heaviness and lightness, must also lack heat and cold. But an objector challenges this, and the response reveals a key principle about causality: the effect can be absent if the substrate is not receptive. This lesson walks through the argument, the objection, and the final resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structured argument: claim, proof, objection, response.
2
Identify the core correlation argument
Ibn Sīnā states that heaviness correlates with coldness and lightness with heat. Since celestial bodies have no heaviness or lightness, they must have no cold or heat.
This is the main proof.
3
Understand the objection
The objector says the correlation is only proven for the elements, not for celestial bodies. So maybe celestial bodies could be hot or cold without becoming light or heavy.
This challenges the universal application.
4
Follow the response and the causal principle
Ibn Sīnā replies that heat is the cause of lightness and cold of heaviness, but the cause may not produce its effect if the matter is not receptive. Just as motion causes heat in elements but not in celestial matter, heat and cold may not produce lightness and heaviness in celestial bodies.
This saves the argument by limiting the correlation to receptive substrates.
5
Reflect on the key takeaway
The lesson shows that causality is not always direct; the nature of the substrate matters. This is a fundamental concept in Aristotelian physics and Islamic theology.
Think about how this principle applies elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن ( أي المحدد، وكذا غيره الأفلاك ) لا حار ولا بارد قال ابن سينا ) وذلك ( لتلازم الثقل مع البرودة ) فإن المادة إذا اشتد بردها ثقلت وإذا ثقلت بردت (و) تلازم (الخفة مع الحرارة ) فإن المادة إذا أمعن فيها التسخين خفت وإذا خفت ،سخنت فحيث لا ثقل ولا خفة فلا برودة ولا حرارة، وقد وقع في بعض النسخ لفظ اليبوسة بدل الحرارة، وهو سهو من القلم ) ولمانع أن يمنع التلازم بين الثقل والبرودة وبين الخفة والحرارة ( مطلقاً بل ) ذلك التلازم ) في العناصر فقط دون الأفلاك، فجاز أن يكون فيها حرارة أو برودة بلا خفة وثقل ( فإن قال : ( ابن سينا ( الحرارة علة (الخفة كما أن البرودة علة الثقل ( فيمتنع التخلف ( فلو وجدنا في الأفلاك لترتب المعلولان عليهما ( قلنا : قد يتخلف الأثر عن العلة الفاعلية لعدم القابل كالحركة فإنها توجب الحرارة في العناصر القابلة لها ) والأفلاك متحركة وغير حارة لأن مادتها غير قابلة للحرارة عندكم فيجوز أن تتخلف الخفة والثقل عن الحرارة والبرودة، لأن مادة الفلك لا تقبلهما، وإن كانتا مقتضيتين لهما .
And among them is that (i.e., the determinate body, and likewise other celestial spheres) is not hot nor cold. Ibn Sīnā said: That is because of the correlation of heaviness with coldness: when matter becomes intensely cold it becomes heavy, and when it becomes heavy it becomes cold; and the correlation of lightness with heat: when matter is intensely heated it becomes light, and when it becomes light it becomes hot. So where there is no heaviness nor lightness, there is no coldness nor heat. And it has occurred in some copies the word 'dryness' instead of 'heat', and it is a slip of the pen. And an objector may object that the correlation between heaviness and coldness and between lightness and heat is not absolute, but rather that correlation is only in the elements, not in the celestial spheres, so it is possible that in them there is heat or coldness without lightness or heaviness. If he says: Ibn Sīnā says heat is the cause of lightness just as coldness is the cause of heaviness, so it is impossible for the effect to be absent [from the cause]; so if we found [those qualities] in the celestial spheres, the two effects would be consequent upon them. We say: The effect may be absent from the efficient cause due to the absence of a receptive substrate, like motion: it causes heat in the elements receptive to it, but the celestial spheres are moving yet not hot because their matter is not receptive to heat according to you [the objector]. So it is possible that lightness and heaviness are absent from heat and coldness, because the matter of the celestial sphere does not receive them, even if they [heat and coldness] necessitate them.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sīnā's proof that celestial bodies (the 'determinate body' and other spheres) are neither hot nor cold. The proof relies on the observed correlation in the sublunar elements: heaviness accompanies coldness, lightness accompanies heat. Since celestial bodies lack both heaviness and lightness (they have only circular motion, no rectilinear motion), they must lack coldness and heat. An objector raises the point that this correlation is only proven in the elements, not necessarily in the celestial realm; perhaps celestial bodies could be hot or cold without becoming light or heavy. The author then has Ibn Sīnā respond: heat is the cause of lightness and coldness the cause of heaviness, so if the effect (lightness/heaviness) is absent, the cause (heat/cold) must also be absent. But the objector persists: causality implies necessary connection. The final reply is that an efficient cause may fail to produce its effect if the receptive substrate is not prepared. Just as motion causes heat in elements but not in celestial matter (according to the opponent's own admission), so heat and coldness might not produce lightness and heaviness if the celestial matter does not accept them. Thus the correlation is not absolute; it holds only in the elements.

Key Terms
تلازم
ثقل
خفة
برودة
حرارة
العناصر
الأفلاك
قابل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the correlation of heaviness/cold and lightness/heat is universal across all types of matter.
Correction: The passage argues that the correlation is contingent on the receptive nature of matter; in celestial matter, heat and cold may exist without corresponding lightness or heaviness because the substrate does not accept those properties.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sīnā's argument in the passage, why can heat exist without lightness in celestial bodies?
A) Because celestial bodies are not made of matter.
B) Because the efficient cause may not produce its effect if the substrate is not receptive.
C) Because heat and lightness are not correlated at all.
D) Because celestial bodies are in circular motion, which prevents lightness.

Key Takeaway
The absence of an effect (lightness/heaviness) does not disprove the presence of its cause (heat/cold) if the matter is not receptive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because a cause exists, the expected result must follow, only to be disappointed? What does this lesson teach you about the role of your own inner receptivity in allowing divine guidance to take effect?
?
If celestial matter is not receptive to heat or cold, what kind of 'matter' is your heart? Are you actively making yourself receptive to truth and virtue, or are you content to remain inert like the spheres?
?
Ibn Sina's argument shows that an effect may be absent even if the cause is present, due to lack of receptivity. What 'effects' of faith (like humility, gratitude, or patience) are absent in your life, and what might that reveal about the state of your heart's receptivity?

Al-Rāzī's Argument and Initial Rebuttals on Celestial Heat
Al-Rāzī's argument and the first three rebuttals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Rāzī's 'reliable argument' that celestial spheres cannot be hot and the author's first three rebuttals concerning degrees of heat, obstructions, and the counterexample of the sun.

Core Idea
Heat is not a single uniform property; celestial matter may only accept weak heat.

Al-Rāzī presents a seemingly airtight argument that if the celestial spheres were hot, they would be maximally hot, but the author responds by showing that heat exists in different types and that obstacles can block it. This lesson walks through the argument and its first three rebuttals.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Rāzī's argument
Read the Arabic passage of the argument carefully. Notice it is a conditional: if spheres were hot, they would be maximally hot due to an efficient cause and a receptive subject free of obstacles. Then the conclusion that this is false is supported by two absurdities.
The argument is called 'al-mu'tamad' by al-Rāzī.
2
Identify the three rebuttals
After the argument, the author responds with three points. Find each: (1) degrees of heat differ in kind, (2) even if strong, the frigid zone blocks, (3) the argument is undermined by the sun example.
These rebuttals are introduced by 'قلنا' and 'ثم إن سلمنا' and 'وهو منقوض'.
3
Reflect on the sun counterexample
The sun is hot, yet its immediate vicinity (mountaintops) is not hotter. This challenges al-Rāzī's claim that if the sphere were hot, the nearest part would be hottest.
This shows the argument is not universally valid.
4
Understand the role of obstacles
The second rebuttal introduces the frigid zone (al-ṭabaqa al-zamharīriyya) as a possible barrier that prevents heat from reaching us. This shows that even if the sphere is hot, its heat may not be perceptible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الإمام الرازي) في المباحث المشرقية : المعتمد في أن الفلك ليس بحار ولا بارد أن يقال : ( لو كانت هي ( أي الأفلاك ) حارة لكانت في غاية الحرارة لوجود الفاعل ) الذي هو طبيعة الفلك ( والقابل ) الذي هو غير عائق ) هناك لكونها بسيطة ) والتالي باطل وإلا كان الأقرب ) من الفلك ( أسخن كرؤوس الجبال الشامخة ولاستحالة ) أي التالي باطل لما ذكر ولاستحالة ( أن تسخن الشمس وحدها حال طلوعها ( دون السماوات ( التي هي في غاية الحرارة ( مع أنها ) أعني السماوات ( أضعاف أضعافها إذ هي فيها كقطرة في بحر لجي . ( قلنا : ) في الجواب عن هذا المعتمد ( مراتب السخونة مختلفة بالنوع فربما لا تقبل مادة الفلك إلا مرتبة ) ما ( ضعيفة ) من الحرارة فلا تؤثر حرارته في عالمنا هذا ( ثم ) إن سلمنا قوة ) حرارتها ( قلنا : ( أثر التسخين) منها ( قد لا يصل إلينا ) لأن الطبقة الزمهريرية مانعة له (وهو) أي الدليل المذكور ( منقوض بتسخين الشمس ) فإنها حارة يصل أثر تسخينها إلى العناصر كما اعترف المستدل به مع أن الأقرب منها ليس أسخن، ثم اعترض المصنف على المعتمد اعتراضاً رابعاً ، وهو قوله : ( والقياس عليها ) أي قياس الأفلاك على تقدير كونها حارة على الشمس في التسخين ) ضعيف لأنها لا تسخن بل أشعتها ) هي المسخنة إذا انعكست من سطوح الأجسام الكثيفة، ولذلك إذا انعكست ) أشعتها من أمور صقيلة جداً ( أحرقت ) الأشياء المنعكس إليها ( كما في المرايا المحرقة ) وليس للأفلاك الحارة بالفرض أشعة تقتضي تسخينا، واعتراضاً خامساً : أعني قوله : ( وما ذكره منقوض بكرة النار لثبوتها عندهم وإحاطتها بسائر العناصر، فلو صح الدليل المعتمد لزم أن لا تكون كرة النار حارة . وقد يقال : الطبقة الزمهريرية تقاومها، ولا يتصور مقاومتها للأفلاك المتسخنة جداً، إذ لا قدر لها بالقياس إليها كما لا يخفى قوله : ( وليس إلخ) سواء كانت مخالفة للأولى في التنوع أو موافقة كما يدل عليه آخر كلام الشارح من قوله : وإن فرض لصورتين متفقتين إلخ .
And Imam al-Rāzī said in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya: The reliable [argument] that the celestial sphere is not hot nor cold is to say: If they (i.e., the celestial spheres) were hot, they would be in the utmost degree of heat because the agent (which is the nature of the sphere) and the recipient (which is not an obstacle, since it is simple) exist. The consequent is false, otherwise the closest [part] of the sphere would be hotter, like the peaks of high mountains; and it is impossible that the sun alone heats at its rising without the heavens which are in utmost heat, while they (the heavens) are many times greater [than the sun], for the sun in them is like a drop in a vast sea. We say, in answer to this reliable argument: The degrees of heat differ in kind; perhaps the matter of the sphere does not accept except a weak degree of heat, so its heat does not affect our world. Then if we grant the strength of its heat, we say: Its heating effect may not reach us because the frigid zone (al-ṭabaqa al-zamharīriyya) prevents it. And this mentioned argument is refuted by the heating of the sun: the sun is hot and its heating effect reaches the elements, as the arguer admits, yet what is closer to it is not hotter. Then the author objected to the reliable argument with a fourth objection, namely his saying: The analogy to it (i.e., analogy of the spheres, supposing they are hot, to the sun in heating) is weak, because [the sun] does not heat; rather its rays heat when reflected from the surfaces of dense bodies. Therefore if its rays are reflected from very polished things, they burn the things reflected upon, as in burning mirrors. And the sphere, if hypothetically hot, does not have rays that necessitate heating. And a fifth objection: namely his saying: What he mentioned is refuted by the sphere of fire, since it is established according to them that it surrounds the other elements. If the reliable argument were correct, it would necessitate that the sphere of fire not be hot. It may be said: the frigid zone resists it, but its resistance to the extremely heated spheres is inconceivable, for it has no power in comparison to them, as is obvious. His saying: (and it is not etc.)—whether it is different from the first in kind or similar, as indicated by the end of the commentator's words: 'and if two forms are assumed identical etc.'
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents al-Rāzī's 'reliable argument' (al-mu'tamad) for the claim that celestial spheres are not hot. The argument: if a sphere were hot, it would be maximally hot because it has both an active cause (its nature) and a receptive subject (its simple matter) without obstacles. The conclusion is false, because the nearest part to us would then be hottest (like mountain peaks), and the sun alone would not heat without the even hotter heavens. The author (the commentator on al-Mawāqif) provides several rebuttals. First, heat levels differ in kind, so the sphere may have a weak heat that does not affect us. Second, even if strong, a cold layer might block its effect. Third, the argument is refuted by the sun: the sun is hot yet its vicinity is not hotter (mountaintops are colder). Fourth, the analogy to the sun is weak because the sun heats only by reflected rays; a hot sphere would have no such rays. Fifth, the argument would imply the sphere of fire (a hot celestial body according to some) would not be hot, which is false. Finally, a marginal note clarifies that the commentator's point about 'not being the same' refers to whether the heat in question is of a different type or not.

Key Terms
المعتمد
الفاعل
القابل
البساطة
مراتب السخونة
الطبقة الزمهريرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Rāzī's argument is conclusive and proves that celestial spheres cannot be hot.
Correction: The author shows that the argument fails because it assumes heat is a single uniform property and ignores obstacles; the sun example alone refutes it.

Checkpoint
What is the second rebuttal the author gives against al-Rāzī's argument?
A) The sphere's heat is weak in degree.
B) The frigid zone may block the heat from reaching us.
C) The sun shows the argument is flawed.
D) The sphere of fire is a counterexample.

Key Takeaway
Al-Rāzī's argument is not conclusive because heat comes in different types and can be blocked, and the sun example undermines the claim that the nearest region must be hottest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an argument that seems logically sound, like al-Rāzī's here, do you tend to accept it immediately, or do you look for hidden assumptions? Are there beliefs about your faith that you hold as 'conclusive' but have never honestly examined from another angle? Commit today to studying one such belief with the same critical eye this author shows.
?
The author points out that heat can be blocked by a 'frigid zone.' In your spiritual life, what 'frigid zones'—habits, distractions, or sins—might be blocking the heat of divine guidance from reaching your heart? What is one cold layer you will remove this week to let the warmth of iman penetrate more deeply?
